    "lag168 <- c(rep(0,168),x$Consumption[1:lag168_end])\n",

    "lin_fit <- lm(Consumption~lag48+lag168, x_train)\n",
    "summary(lin_fit)"

    "# Part C"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "raw",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "As mentioned earlier, hourly seasonality is important. Although we used the same hour’s consumption value of the past days to handle this problem for part (b), we implicitly impose an assumption that prediction model for each hour has the same coefficients which may not be correct since the consumption behavior at nights can be different than the other hours. Therefore, modeling each hour separately is another way to approach to the same problem. Train linear regression models for each hour using the same training period (24 models) and report your test performance as in part (a).\n"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "raw",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "Now we will check each hour seperately. Similar to what we did in part (b). Only we now create a for loop for each hour and use the data from the same hour for training."
   ]
  },

   "source": [
    "res_mape <- data.frame('Hours'=integer(),\n",
    "                 'MAPE'=double())\n",
    "for (t in c(0:23)){\n",
    "    x_temp <- x[x$Time == t,]\n",
    "    x_temp_train <- x_temp[x_temp$Date < '2020-11-01',]\n",
    "    x_temp_test <- x_temp[x_temp$Date >= '2020-11-01',]\n",
    "    fit_temp <- lm(Consumption~lag48+lag168,x_temp_train)\n",
    "    x_temp_pred <- predict(fit_temp,x_temp_test)\n",
    "    res_mape <- rbind(res_mape,c(t,MAPE(x_temp_pred,x_temp_test$Consumption)))\n",
    "}\n",
    "colnames(res_mape) <- c('Hour', 'MAPE')\n",
    "res_mape"
   ]
  },

    "#  Part d"
   ]

  {
   "cell_type": "raw",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "One of your friends comes up with an alternative approach assuming that all hourly consumption values of last week (same day) can be important in the prediction of the next day’s consumption. In other words, you can use the 24 consumption values of the last week to predict next day’s consumption. This requires the transformation of your data into a so called “wide” format from the “long” format (i.e. the form of the data you used in part (a) is referred to as “long” format). Figure 2 illustrates the wide format for predicting the consumption of 18th hour.\n",
    "\n",
    "Assume that you have 48 features (hourly consumption from two days ago and last week’s hourly consumption) in total. You are also willing to follow the same logic in part (c) and build a prediction model for each hour separately. Since there is a strong correlation (actually an autocorrelation) between these predictors, you are willing to use penalized regression approaches for modeling. Use L1 penalty in your regression models for each hour. Note that the feature matrix will be the same for all your models, only the target variable will change for this task. In order to determine the regularization parameter (i.e. lambda), perform a 10-fold cross-validation.\n",
    "\n",
    "Train penalized regression models with L1 penalty (i.e. lasso regression) for each hour using the same training period (24 models) and report your test performance as in part (a). Also comment on the resulting models (i.e. coefficients and etc.)."
   ]

    "m <- data.frame()\n",
    "for (date_index in 1:length(unique_dates)){\n",
    "    date <- as.Date(unique_dates[date_index],origin='2020-11-24')\n",
    "    m <- rbind(m, cbind.data.frame(date, t(x[x$Date == date,]$lag168), t(x[x$Date == date,]$lag48), x[x$Date == date,][x$Time == 0,]$Consumption[1]))\n",
    "}\n",
    "for (j in c(2:25)){\n",
    "    colnames(m)[j] <- paste('Lag_day7_hour',j-2,sep='_')\n",
    "}\n",
    "for (j in c(26:49)){\n",
    "    colnames(m)[j] <- paste('Lag_day2_hour',j-26,sep='_')\n",
    "}\n",
    "colnames(m)[50] <- 'Consumption_0'\n",
    "colnames(m)[1] <- 'Date'\n",
    "\n",
    "m"
   ]
